            function hasConnection() {
                return navigator.connection || navigator.mozConnection || navigator.webkitConnection || navigator.msConnection;
            }

            function createLink(src) {
                var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                preloadLink.href = src;
                preloadLink.as = "font";
                preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
            }

            var connectionDetail = {
                saveDataEnabled: false,
                slowConnection: false
            };

            var connection = hasConnection();
            if (connection) {
                connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled = connection.saveData;
                if (/\slow-2g|2g/.test(connection.effectiveType)) {
                    connectionDetail.slowConnection = true;
                }
            }

            if (!(connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled || connectionDetail.slowConnection)) {
                createLink("/static/fonts/HardingText-Regular-Web-cecd90984f.woff2");
            } else {
                document.documentElement.classList.add('save-data');
            }

    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Bridging multiscale interfaces for developing ionically conductive high-voltage iron sulfate-containing sodium-based battery positive electrodes","description":"Non-aqueous sodium-ion batteries (SiBs) are a viable electrochemical energy storage system for grid storage. However, the practical development of SiBs is hindered mainly by the sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability of positive-electrode active materials, such as polyanion-type iron-based sulfates, at high voltage. Here, to circumvent these issues, we proposed the multiscale interface engineering of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3, where bulk heterostructure and exposed crystal plane were tuned to improve the Na-ion storage performance. Physicochemical characterizations and theoretical calculations suggested that the heterostructure of Na6Fe(SO4)4 phase facilitated ionic kinetics by densifying Na-ion migration channels and lowering energy barriers. The (11-2) plane of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3 promoted the adsorption of the electrolyte solution ClO4− anions and fluoroethylene carbonate molecules, which formed an inorganic-rich Na-ion conductive interphase at the positive electrode.     <meta name="dc.title" content="Bridging multiscale interfaces for developing ionically conductive high-voltage iron sulfate-containing sodium-based battery positive electrodes">
    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2023 14:1">
    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html">
    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group">
    <meta name="dc.date" content="2023-06-22">

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com">
    <meta name="dc.description" content="Non-aqueous sodium-ion batteries (SiBs) are a viable electrochemical energy storage system for grid storage. However, the practical development of SiBs is hindered mainly by the sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability of positive-electrode active materials, such as polyanion-type iron-based sulfates, at high voltage. Here, to circumvent these issues, we proposed the multiscale interface engineering of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3, where bulk heterostructure and exposed crystal plane were tuned to improve the Na-ion storage performance. Physicochemical characterizations and theoretical calculations suggested that the heterostructure of Na6Fe(SO4)4 phase facilitated ionic kinetics by densifying Na-ion migration channels and lowering energy barriers. The (11-2) plane of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3 promoted the adsorption of the electrolyte solution ClO4− anions and fluoroethylene carbonate molecules, which formed an inorganic-rich Na-ion conductive interphase at the positive electrode. When tested in combination with a presodiated FeS/carbon-based negative electrode in laboratory- scale single-layer pouch cell configuration, the Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3-based positive electrode enables an initial discharge capacity of about 83.9 mAh g−1, an average cell discharge voltage of 2.35 V and a specific capacity retention of around 97% after 40 cycles at 24 mA g−1 and 25 °C. Sluggish kinetics is a major challenge for iron-based sulfate electrode materials. Here, the authors report multiscale interface engineering to build continuous Na-ion transfer channels at all length scales by designing ionic conductors inside bulk and low-electron-density exposed crystal surfaces.">

    <meta name="description" content="Non-aqueous sodium-ion batteries (SiBs) are a viable electrochemical energy storage system for grid storage. However, the practical development of SiBs is hindered mainly by the sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability of positive-electrode active materials, such as polyanion-type iron-based sulfates, at high voltage. Here, to circumvent these issues, we proposed the multiscale interface engineering of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3, where bulk heterostructure and exposed crystal plane were tuned to improve the Na-ion storage performance. Physicochemical characterizations and theoretical calculations suggested that the heterostructure of Na6Fe(SO4)4 phase facilitated ionic kinetics by densifying Na-ion migration channels and lowering energy barriers. The (11-2) plane of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3 promoted the adsorption of the electrolyte solution ClO4− anions and fluoroethylene carbonate molecules, which formed an inorganic-rich Na-ion conductive interphase at the positive electrode. When tested in combination with a presodiated FeS/carbon-based negative electrode in laboratory- scale single-layer pouch cell configuration, the Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3-based positive electrode enables an initial discharge capacity of about 83.9 mAh g−1, an average cell discharge voltage of 2.35 V and a specific capacity retention of around 97% after 40 cycles at 24 mA g−1 and 25 °C. Sluggish kinetics is a major challenge for iron-based sulfate electrode materials. Here, the authors report multiscale interface engineering to build continuous Na-ion transfer channels at all length scales by designing ionic conductors inside bulk and low-electron-density exposed crystal surfaces.">

        <div class="c-article-body">
            <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Non-aqueous sodium-ion batteries (SiBs) are a viable electrochemical energy storage system for grid storage. However, the practical development of SiBs is hindered mainly by the sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability of positive-electrode active materials, such as polyanion-type iron-based sulfates, at high voltage. Here, to circumvent these issues, we proposed the multiscale interface engineering of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, where bulk heterostructure and exposed crystal plane were tuned to improve the Na-ion storage performance. Physicochemical characterizations and theoretical calculations suggested that the heterostructure of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase facilitated ionic kinetics by densifying Na-ion migration channels and lowering energy barriers. The (11-2) plane of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> promoted the adsorption of the electrolyte solution ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and fluoroethylene carbonate molecules, which formed an inorganic-rich Na-ion conductive interphase at the positive electrode. When tested in combination with a presodiated FeS/carbon-based negative electrode in laboratory- scale single-layer pouch cell configuration, the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-based positive electrode enables an initial discharge capacity of about 83.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, an average cell discharge voltage of 2.35 V and a specific capacity retention of around 97% after 40 cycles at 24 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 25 °C.</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="main-content">
                    
                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Environmental pollution and limited fossil energy push the development of renewable clean energies. However, their intermittent and regional features, which are prioritized at the forefront and in projects of countries, require large-scale energy storage systems to function together<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Zhao, C. et al. Rational design of layered oxide materials for sodium-ion batteries. Science 370, 708–712 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e550">1</a></sup>. Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SiBs), as one of the most promising next-generation energy storage technology, have introduced a new opportunity due to the abundant Na reserves and their worldwide distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Li, Y. et al. Interfacial engineering to achieve an energy density of over 200 Wh kg−1 in sodium batteries. Nat. Energy 7, 511–519 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e554">2</a></sup>. The development of high-voltage (&gt;3.6 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and resource-abundant cathodes with long operating life to circumvent raw and cost limitations is progressing rapidly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wang, Z. et al. Efficient potential-tuning strategy through p-type doping for designing cathodes with ultrahigh energy density. Natl Sci. Rev. 7, 1768–1775 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e560">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Stallard, J. C. et al. Mechanical properties of cathode materials for lithium-ion batteries. Joule 6, 984–1007 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e563">4</a></sup>. In this regard, alluaudite-type iron-based sulfates are considered promising positive-electrode active material candidates due to their abundant resources, high voltage (3.8 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and robust polyanion frameworks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Barpanda, P., Oyama, G., Nishimura, S., Chung, S.-C. &amp; Yamada, A. A 3.8-V earth-abundant sodium battery electrode. Nat. Commun. 5, 4358 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e570">5</a></sup>. However, the large bandgap hinders the rapid transfer and reaction kinetics of charge carriers (Na<sup>+</sup> and electron), which results in the poor cycling reversibility and rate performance of batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jin, T. et al. Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Chem. Soc. Rev. 49, 2342–2377 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e576">6</a></sup>. Furthermore, electrolytes suffer from severe oxidative decomposition at high voltages, which causes the formation of a solid interphase, that is, the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tan, S. et al. Additive engineering for robust interphases to stabilize high-Ni layered structures at ultra-high voltage of 4.8 V. Nat. Energy 7, 484–494 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e580">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chen, J. et al. Electrolyte design for LiF-rich solid–electrolyte interfaces to enable high-performance microsized alloy anodes for batteries. Nat. Energy 5, 386–397 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e583">8</a></sup>. CEI influences Na<sup>+</sup> insertion from the solvated phase into the solid phase, and thus constitutes an extra rate-limiting step for sodiation of most cathodes.</p><p>Highly connected ion-conducting pathways within the cathode may provide an effective strategy to circumvent the above problems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qian, J. et al. Boosting fast sodium storage of a large-scalable carbon anode with an ultralong cycle life. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1703159 (2018)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e592">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, J. et al. Raising the intrinsic safety of layered oxide cathodes by surface re‐lithiation with LLZTO garnet‐type solid electrolytes. Adv. Mater. 34, 2200655 (2022)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e592_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Meng, W., Dang, Z., Li, D., Jiang, L. &amp; Fang, D. Interface and defect engineered titanium‐base oxide heterostructures synchronizing high‐rate and ultrastable sodium storage. Adv. Energy Mater. 12, 2201531 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e595">11</a></sup>. Their construction requires the consideration of all essential components in the positive electrode, including particles of active materials and their compatibility with electrolytes at the cell level, because ion-conducting pathways are distributed at all length scales, from Na<sup>+</sup> migration within crystalline lattices or boundaries to Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation or dislocation within the CEI at the cathode interface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liang, H. et al. Ether‐based electrolyte chemistry towards high‐voltage and long‐life Na‐ion full batteries. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 60, 26837–26846 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e603">12</a></sup>. For the bulk design of particles, nano-engineering of cathode materials is usually used to decrease the ionic transfer distance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, X. et al. Rapid mechanochemical synthesis of polyanionic cathode with improved electrochemical performance for Na-ion batteries. Nat. Commun. 12, 2848 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e607">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Park, S. et al. Phase-pure Na3V2(PO4)2F3 embedded in carbon matrix through a facile polyol synthesis as a potential cathode for high performance sodium-ion batteries. Nano Res. 12, 911–917 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e610">14</a></sup>. However, the effect is upper-limited by electrode materials, which cannot accommodate the Na<sup>+</sup> rapidly passing through during solid-solution transformation due to large kinetic barriers and limited migration channels available inside the crystals. The introduction of additional materials with high ionic conductivity was used to facilitate the ionic kinetics of composite cathodes, such as the tailored heterostructure in oxide cathodes, through the incorporation of semiconductor components with different bandgaps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Liang, X., Yu, T. Y., Ryu, H. H. &amp; Sun, Y. K. Hierarchical O3/P2 heterostructured cathode materials for advanced sodium-ion batteries. Energy Storage Mater. 47, 515–525 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e617">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Zhu, Y. et al. Manipulating layered P2@P3 integrated spinel structure evolution for high‐performance sodium‐ion batteries. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 59, 9299–9304 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e620">16</a></sup>. However, the chemical construction of heterostructures in positive electrodes with complex polyanion frameworks has not been reported and, is subject to harsh synthesis conditions such as relatively low temperature (&lt;350 <sup>o</sup>C) and uncontrollable solid-phase reaction thermodynamics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Dwibedi, D., Barpanda, P. &amp; Yamada, A. Alluaudite battery cathodes. Small Methods 4, 2000051 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e626">17</a></sup>. In this sense, the heterostructure design of bulk materials still needs to be explored to obtain satisfactory performance on high-voltage cathodes.</p><p>The primary construction of CEI is intrinsically dependent on electrochemical reactions of the electrolyte on the cathode surface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Cathode-electrolyte interphase in lithium batteries revealed by cryogenic electron microscopy. Matter 4, 302–312 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e633">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fan, X. &amp; Wang, C. High-voltage liquid electrolytes for Li batteries: progress and perspectives. Chem. Soc. Rev. 50, 10486–10566 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e636">19</a></sup>. Typically, constrained by the strong surface nucleophilicity of the cathode, solvents based on ethylene carbonate are preferentially absorbed, and the derived oligomers (e.g., polycarbonate) constitute a thick and ionically insulating CEI through nucleophilic reactions, which increases the interfacial impedance and battery polarization. Thus, the exposed crystal planes of cathode materials, which are closely related to their electronic structure and atomic properties, are one of the key parameters to manipulate the CEI formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zheng, J. &amp; Archer, L. A. Crystallographically textured electrodes for rechargeable batteries: symmetry, fabrication, and characterization. Chem. Rev. 122, 14440–14470 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e640">20</a></sup>. However, the influence on CEI formation of the cathodes, specifically their exposed crystal planes and electric structures, is not fully investigated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, Y. et al. How can the electrode influence the formation of the solid electrolyte interface? ACS Energy Lett. 6, 3307–3320 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e644">21</a></sup>.</p><p>In this work, multiscale interfaces were successfully integrated in high-voltage iron-based sulfate cathodes to build continuous Na<sup>+</sup> transfer channels at all length scales. Specifically, the ionic kinetics of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> inside the particle bulk was improved by introducing minor ionic-conductive Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase with dense Na<sup>+</sup> migration channels and low barriers. The rapid ionic transfer across the cathode/electrolyte interface and electrolyte stability at high voltage were achieved via an inorganic-rich and uniform CEI, which was induced by the (11-2) plane exposure of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with a low-electron density. Their synergy qualitatively improved the ionic kinetics and reversibility of the positive-electrode active material, enabling its successful application in lab-scale iron sulfur-based Na-ion cells to demonstrate the scalability of these class of materials to up to a technology readiness level of 4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Frith, J. T., Lacey, M. J. &amp; Ulissi, U. A non-academic perspective on the future of lithium-based batteries. Nat. Commun. 14, 420 (2023)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e679">22</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>A sodium-ion-conducting high-voltage cathode Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase with a Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase heterostructure and an electronically conductive carbon network (named NFS-H) was synthesized via co-precipitation and subsequent calcination (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">1</a>). Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and FeSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved and coprecipitated into hydrated sulfate (Na<sub>2</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O) as intermediate during the alcohol-induced precipitation and another intermediate thermally decomposed into off-stoichiometric Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with a heterostructure of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> through thermal competition reactions due to its local atomic inhomogeneity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Meng, Y., Yu, T., Zhang, S. &amp; Deng, C. Top-down synthesis of muscle-inspired alluaudite Na2+2xFe2-x(SO4)3/SWNT spindle as a high-rate and high-potential cathode for sodium-ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 4, 1624–1631 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e742">23</a></sup>. Therefore, heterostructured phases can be generated synchronously with close contact (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1a</a>). Importantly, the dominant crystal plane of the intermediate (Na<sub>2</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O) featured a high similarity to the (11-2) plane of Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in atomic plane arrangement, which eased the transformation of the former into the latter atomic arrangements through topological reactions. (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">2</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Xiong, S. et al. Computation-guided design of LiTaSiO5, a new lithium ionic conductor with sphene structure. Adv. Energy Mater. 9, 1803821 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e769">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fu, F. et al. Entropy and crystal-facet modulation of P2-type layered cathodes for long-lasting sodium-based batteries. Nat. Commun. 13, 2826 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e772">25</a></sup>. By calculating the various dominant crystal plane of Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> crystals, we determined that the (11-2) plane had a relatively loose atomic arrangement and the lowest electron density with surface energy (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">2</a>). Thus, the obtained Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> may be more likely to expose the (11-2) crystal plane, and the products directly converted from Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and FeSO<sub>4</sub> exhibited weaker diffraction intensities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, M. et al. A novel graphene oxide wrapped Na2Fe2(SO4)3/C cathode composite for long life and high energy density sodium‐ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1800944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e804">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pan, W. et al. Na2Fe(SO4)2: an anhydrous 3.6 V, low-cost and good-safety cathode for a rechargeable sodium-ion battery. J. Mater. Chem. A 7, 13197–13204 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e807">27</a></sup>. NFS-H particles featured micron-sized (6 <b>−</b> 8 μm) polyhedra inherited from the intermediate, and they consisted of stacked nanoparticles and multilayer reduced graphene oxide sheets attached to the surface (the inset in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">3</a>). The micron-sized particles with a low surface area (8.15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and meso- and microporous structures allowed structural robustness and sufficient electrolyte penetration (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">4</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Sing, K. S. W. Reporting physisorption data for gas/solid systems with special reference to the determination of surface area and porosity (Recommendations 1984). Pure Appl. Chem. 57, 603–619 (1985)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e828">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Structural characterizations and Na+ transfer investigation of as-synthesized heterostructured NFS-H."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Structural characterizations and Na<sup>+</sup> transfer investigation of as-synthesized heterostructured NFS-H.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="418"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Schematic illustration of the heterostructure. The blue and red areas represent Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> in positive material, respectively. The white and black arrows represent sodium-ion transport in Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, respectively. And the blue and red arrows represent the characteristics of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (higher working voltage) and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> (faster ionic diffusion), respectively. <b>b</b> Synchrotron high-pressure powder X-ray diffraction Rietveld refinement, which was carried out on NFS-H powder (inset: SEM image. The color of light blue was obtained via additional manipulation in the original SEM image for better color contrast). <b>c</b>, <b>d</b> The Na<sup>+</sup> migration channels (blue-green isosurface) revealed by BVSE calculation for Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> crystals, respectively. <b>e</b> Na<sup>+</sup> migration barriers along different directions. <b>f</b> Energy profiles of path Na2–Na2 along 1D direction.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>All the diffraction peaks in the synchrotron high-pressure powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of NFS-H (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1b</a>) can be assigned to the alluaudite-type Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and vanthoffite-type Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, in which the sharp diffraction peaks indicated a high crystallinity. Notably, compared with Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> which was converted directly from the composite of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and FeSO<sub>4</sub> as reported in literature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, M. et al. A novel graphene oxide wrapped Na2Fe2(SO4)3/C cathode composite for long life and high energy density sodium‐ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1800944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e973">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Pan, W. et al. Na2Fe(SO4)2: an anhydrous 3.6 V, low-cost and good-safety cathode for a rechargeable sodium-ion battery. J. Mater. Chem. A 7, 13197–13204 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e976">27</a></sup>, the intermediate-derived Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> showed a relatively stronger (11-2) peak but weaker (200) peaks. Moreover, the contents of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> were adjusted to 90.67 and 9.33 wt% by Rietveld refinement, respectively (detailed crystallographic parameters in Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">4</a>). Within the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> crystal, all the Na<sup>+</sup> can be assigned to three types (Na1, Na2, and Na3) based on their individual binding energies, whereas the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub> dimer is composed of two edge-sharing FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and SO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra units through corner-sharing oxygen atoms (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">5</a>). Meanwhile, for the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> crystal, the host lattice consisted of FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and SO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra through corner-sharing oxygen atoms, in the voids containing three types of Na sites. The similar physicochemical properties of elements and crystal unit parts in the two phases, especially the identical polyhedrons in their frameworks, offer a great possibility for heterostructures during synchronous configuration. For comparison, a Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material containing the less ionic-conductive Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase (4.52 wt%) was synthesized (named as NFS-L, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">6</a>) by promoting the single-phase precipitated FeSO<sub>4</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O to regulate the Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>-FeSO<sub>4</sub> reaction balance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Oyama, G., Nishimura, S., Suzuki, Y., Okubo, M. &amp; Yamada, A. Off-stoichiometry in alluaudite-type sodium iron sulfate Na2+2xFe2−x(SO4)3 as an advanced sodium battery cathode material. ChemElectroChem 2, 1019–1023 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1073">29</a></sup>. However, given the uncontrollable thermodynamics and metastable properties, pure Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> materials were difficult to obtain from the intermediate.</p><p>To gain insights into the intrinsic properties of heterostructured phases in NFS-H in ionic transfer kinetics, we macroscopically probed Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathways by bond valence site energy (BVSE) calculation and visualized them as regions enclosed by isosurfaces. The calculation workflow was managed by the high-throughput computational platform for battery materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="He, B. et al. A highly efficient and informative method to identify ion transport networks in fast ion conductors. Acta Mater. 203, 116490 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1097">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="He, B. et al. CAVD, towards better characterization of void space for ionic transport analysis. Sci. Data 7, 153 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1100">31</a></sup>. Visibly, the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> crystal provided intermittent and narrow channels for Na<sup>+</sup> migration along the b and c-axis directions across a zigzag path between two equivalent positions (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">7</a>). The punctate one-dimensional (1D) pathways limited the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> migration inside the crystal, which may account for the poor ionic conductivity of Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials. Differently, the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> crystal possessed successive and broad Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathways along the 3D (a, b, and c-axis) directions (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1d</a>). Further calculations of the energy barrier revealed that Na<sup>+</sup> migrated mainly along the 1D and 2D directions in Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> crystals, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1e</a>). Notably, except for the richer 3D Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels inside the crystals, Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> featured a lower barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> migration within these pathways in different directions. With the 1D migration pathway as an example, the Na2–Na2 transfer displayed a lower energy barrier of 1.29 eV in the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase, compared with that in the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase (3.52 eV, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig1">1f</a>). The calculation result from another calculation software (softBV) displayed similar trend but lower barrier values (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">8</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">1</a>). Furthermore, the determined diffusion coefficients of the as-prepared Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase ranged from 10<sup>−10.54</sup> to 10<sup>−7.21</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at 25 °C (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">9</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">2</a>), which were higher than those of pure alluaudite-type Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, M. et al. A novel graphene oxide wrapped Na2Fe2(SO4)3/C cathode composite for long life and high energy density sodium‐ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1800944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1229">26</a></sup>. Therefore, the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase, which has been widely regarded as an impurity during the synthesis of Na<sub>2+2x</sub>Fe<sub>2-x</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Oyama, G., Nishimura, S., Suzuki, Y., Okubo, M. &amp; Yamada, A. Off-stoichiometry in alluaudite-type sodium iron sulfate Na2+2xFe2−x(SO4)3 as an advanced sodium battery cathode material. ChemElectroChem 2, 1019–1023 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1248">29</a></sup>, may act as a superionic conductor in bulk materials, which enhances the ionic conductivity and reaction kinetics of the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase.</p><p>The morphology and heterostructure of NFS-H were revealed by high-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscope (HAADF-STEM) images. By intense ultrasonic treatment on NFS-H, the separated nanoparticles with a trapezoidal-like morphology were obtained (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">10</a>), in which a lattice fringe spacing of 0.361 nm pointed to the (13-1) plane of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Meanwhile, a well-designed carbon network was observed around the nanoparticles. Based on thermal gravimetric analysis, the carbon content in NFS-H reached 12.64 wt%, which indicated a high-degree graphitization that was beneficial for electronic conductivity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Song, K. et al. Atomically dispersed Ni induced by ultrahigh N-doped carbon enables stable sodium storage. Chem 7, 2684–2694 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1276">32</a></sup>. The HAADF-STEM images further identified the fine crystal fringes spread inside the NFS-H particle, following the highly crystalline feature revealed by the XRD pattern (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2a</a>). The lattice fringes of 0.277 and 0.308 nm matched well the (240) and (11-2) planes of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, respectively, and abundant (11-2) planes were exposed at the periphery of particles. However, other crystal planes such as the (240), (13-1), and (200), were also observed (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">11</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">3</a>). Furthermore, the heterostructure between Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> with close contact was visible and with evident grain boundaries, in which the lattice fringes of 0.341 and 0.358 nm matched well the (21-2) plane of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> and the (13-1) plane of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2b</a>). The corresponding “bright-bright-dark” arrangements resolved the two types of bright spot arrangement (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2c</a>), which was also characterized by scan lines and fast Fourier-transform (FFT) patterns (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">12</a>). Furthermore, the element mappings indicated the coexistence and uniform distribution of Na, Fe, S, and O in NFS-H particles, which is in good agreement with their tight combination. (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">3</a>). The atomic ratio of Na:Fe (1.42:1), which was determined by energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) measurements (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">13</a>), was close to the value (1.47:1) calculated from the refined XRD result, which supports the analysis of composition contents. Nevertheless, the difference between the Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phases cannot be determined by Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">14</a>) due to the similar physicochemical properties of their elements and groups.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Structure evolution of NFS-H during the electrochemical process."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Structure evolution of NFS-H during the electrochemical process.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="759"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>HAADF-STEM images at <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> pristine state, <b>c</b> enlarged image of <b>b</b>, <b>d</b> charged 4.5 V, and <b>e</b> discharged 2.0 V upon initial cycle. The blue shadows represent Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase materials. Two Fe arrangements are identified, which are characterized by bright Fe atoms from well-known high atomic numbers. The top-left region with a sparse Fe arrangement corresponds to Fe-less Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, and the bottom-right region with a dense Fe arrangement corresponds to Fe-rich Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <b>f</b> Scheme of Na<sup>+</sup> transfer process. <b>g</b> In situ XRD patterns. <b>h</b> Ex situ XRD patterns at various voltages. The terms of <b>c</b> and <b>d</b> represent the charging and discharging process, respectively, and the values is the voltage at which the Na||NFS-H coin cells were stopped, disconnected, and disassembled for sample collection. The six-point star symbol stands for the (21-2) plane of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To reveal the structural evolution of heterostructured phases inside the cathode bulk, we studied the ex situ HAADF-STEM images at different electrochemical states (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2</a>d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">e</a>). At a charge of 4.5 V (SOC, 100%), the lattice spacing of (21-2) plane in Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> decreased and then returned to nearly the original value at a discharge of 2.0 V (SOC, 0%). This variation indicates that the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase involved electrochemical reactions during the sodiation process (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2f</a>). The ex situ and in situ XRD patterns further demonstrated their structural evolution (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">2</a>g, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig2">h</a>). During the charging process of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, a minor shift to a higher degree marked the emergence of the desodiated Na<sub>6-x</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase, which continued the shift trend until 3.5 V. Afterward, characteristic reflections of desodiated Na<sub>2.26-x</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> appeared with the canting of lattice planes, including the (13-1), (11-2), (400) and (041) planes. Notable, at the beginning of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> desodiation, the diffraction peak of Na<sub>6-x</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> shifted toward a lower degree during the charging process, which indicated the partial Na<sup>+</sup> sodiation from Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to Na<sub>6-x</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. During the subsequent electrochemical process, the peaks retained a gentle evolution trend and finally returned to the initial peak position upon the complete sodiation of Na<sub>2.26-x</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. The ex situ XPS spectra also suggested a similar change in the chemistry and reaction reversibility, which confirmed the solid-solution transition mechanism (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">15</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Fang, Y. et al. An advanced low-cost cathode composed of graphene-coated Na2.4Fe1.8(SO4)3 nanograins in a 3D graphene network for ultra-stable sodium storage. J. Energy Chem. 54, 564–570 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1546">33</a></sup>. Rich Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels were maintained inside the partially desodiated Na<sub>6-x</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, as demonstrated by BVSE calculation (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">16</a>). The results showed that, the heterostructured Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase and its partially desodiated phase played a crucial role in Na<sup>+</sup> conduction during the (de)sodiation process of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and finally contributed to the ionic transfer and reaction reversibility of NHS-H cathode by utilizing high-throughput ion channels and low-energy barriers.</p><p>Ionic-transfer kinetics was strongly associated with the interphase structure and components in the bulk structures, and highly active cathode surfaces produce poorly ionically conductive and thick CEI, which can hamper the fast and reversible Na<sup>+</sup> transfer across the interface (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig3">3a</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Liu, Y. et al. Self-assembled monolayers direct a LiF-rich interphase toward long-life lithium metal batteries. Science 375, 739–745 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1587">34</a></sup>. To reveal the effect of the cathode surface on the formation of optimized CEI, we conducted density functional theory (DFT) calculation on the (11-2) and (200) planes of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> due to their peak variation during the intermediate-involved synthesis. Meanwhile, considering that the inorganic species within CEI, which was mainly derived from the fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) electrolyte additive and (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) salt-derived anions, largely determine the uniformity, robustness, and ionic conductivity, their adsorption and decomposition behaviors in the different crystal planes were studied. Compared with the (200) plane, the (11-2) plane featured less electron density and stronger nucleophilicity due to the loose atom arrangement (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig3">3b</a>). In terms of electrolyte nucleophilic reaction on the electrode surface, the high electron density of crystal planes resulted in a stronger electron-donating capability, which decreased the barrier in the decomposition of the non-aqueous electrolyte solution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fan, X. &amp; Wang, C. High-voltage liquid electrolytes for Li batteries: progress and perspectives. Chem. Soc. Rev. 50, 10486–10566 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1606">19</a></sup>. Thus, the FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> on the (11-2) plane displayed higher bond rupture energies (C–O and C–C bonds; Cl–O bond, respectively) compared with those on the (200) plane, which implied a stronger oxidation resistance (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig3">3</a>c, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig3">d</a>). Furthermore, the FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> with strong polar groups (F and O) featured stronger adsorption behaviors on less-electron-dense (11-2) plane with smaller adsorption distances, which indicated the improved access to the cathode surface and contributed to the formation of FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-rich electric double layer (EDL)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Lin, S., Chen, X. &amp; Wang, Z. L. Contact electrification at the liquid–solid interface. Chem. Rev. 122, 5209–5232 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1627">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Wu, J. Understanding the electric double-layer structure, capacitance, and charging dynamics. Chem. Rev. 122, 10821–10859 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1630">36</a></sup>. Once the cathode reached an appropriate positive potential, the nearest FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> preferentially transferred electrons to the cathode and underwent oxidative decomposition, regardless of the high oxidation resistance. Accordingly, the CEI formed on the cathode with the more exposed (11-2) plane was expected to feature-rich inorganic species and ensure sufficient compactness and ionic conductivity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, J., Gai, J., Song, K. &amp; Chen, W. Advances in electrode/electrolyte interphase for sodium-ion batteries from half cells to full cells. Cell Rep. Phys. Sci. 3, 100868 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1637">37</a></sup>. Meanwhile, the enhanced oxidation resistance of the electrolyte can strengthen further the stability of the cathode/electrolyte interface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Hu, Y. et al. Cyclic-anion salt for high-voltage stable potassium-metal batteries. Natl Sci. Rev. 9, nwac134 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1641">38</a></sup>. These findings firmly indicate the capability of the cathode’s surface properties to regulate the interfacial behavior of electrolytes. The specific exposed crystal plane of the cathode material (e.g., (11-2) plane of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) favored the strong adsorption of several electrolyte components (e.g., FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) and effective construction of the target EDL, which ensured a distinctive inorganic-rich CEI to stabilize the cathode/electrolyte interface at high voltages.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Interface chemistry on NFS-H-based positive electrode."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Interface chemistry on NFS-H-based positive electrode.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="613"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Scheme of interfacial adsorption and CEI formation. The blue flat column represents the positive active material, and the ball on the right represents the ions and solvent molecules in the electrolyte. The small flat panel in the middle represents the CEI produced by the decomposition of the electrolyte. The green and red areas on the left represent organic and inorganic species within the CEI, respectively, and the dashed lines in white represent the transport paths of sodium ions within the CEI. <b>b</b> Electron density distributions of (11-2) and (200) planes of Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, respectively. <b>c</b> Interfacial adsorption and decomposed behaviors of electrolyte components (FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>-</sup>) on different cathode crystal planes with corresponding adsorption distances and bond rupture energies. <b>d</b> Calculated models. Blue, dark gray, light yellow, lilac, gray, light pink, green, and water balls represent Na, Fe, S, O, C, H, Cl, and F atoms, respectively. The sticks represent the chemical bonds between atoms, and the red dotted line represents the positions where the bond breaks.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>CEI, as the passivation layer on the cathode surface, determines the ionic transfer and compatibility of the cathode/electrolyte interface, especially at high voltages. From the ex situ XPS full-spectrum measurements of the positive electrode (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig4">4a</a>), the element contents (Na, O, C, F, and Cl) changed in the etching process, with the F and Cl preserved after 90 s etching, which indicates the presence of residual CEI on the cathode surface. In addition, the highs contents of F (14.11 at.%) and Cl (3.63 at.%) implied the preferred decomposition of FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions. The C 1<i>s</i> spectrum showed the C-C/C-H, C-O, and -OCO<sub>2</sub>- peaks, which indicated standard CEI components, such as sodium alkyl carbonate, sodium carbonate (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), and polyester (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">17</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yao, G. et al. Facile synthesis of hierarchical Na2Fe(SO4)2@rGO/C as high-voltage cathode for energy density-enhanced sodium-ion batteries. J. Energy Chem. 50, 387–394 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1723">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, W. et al. Engineering a passivating electric double layer for high performance lithium metal batteries. Nat. Commun. 13, 2029 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1726">40</a></sup>. Similarly, the O 1<i>s</i> spectrum exhibited strong peaks of C=O, Na auger, C-O/O-H, and Na<sub>2</sub>O. Strong NaF and C-F peaks in the F 1<i>s</i> spectrum were derived from the oxidation of FEC, and NaCl was the characteristic decomposition product of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>. Through Ar<sup>+</sup> etching treatment, the peak intensities, including C-C/C-H, C-F, and -OCO<sub>2</sub>, gradually decreased, which illustrated the principal distributions of organic species (C-C/C=C, C-F species, RCOO<sub>2</sub>, and polyesters) in the outer CEI. Inorganic Na<sub>2</sub>O appeared after 30 s etching, whereas NaF and NaCl were distributed throughout the whole depth, which implied the rich inorganic components in CEI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="He, Y. et al. Progressive growth of the solid–electrolyte interphase towards the Si anode interior causes capacity fading. Nat. Nanotechnol. 16, 1113–1120 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1751">41</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Ex situ physicochemical characterizations of the NFS-H-based positive electrodes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Ex situ physicochemical characterizations of the NFS-H-based positive electrodes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-023-39384-7/MediaObjects/41467_2023_39384_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="564"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The electrodes were tested in Na||NFS-H coin cells at 12 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and the cell was stopped at 4.5 V during the first charge cycle before disassembly. <b>a</b> In-depth element distributions and high-resolution XPS spectra of O 1<i>s</i>, F 1<i>s</i>, Cl 2<i>p</i>. <b>b</b> HAADF-STEM image. <b>c</b> Cryo-TEM images. <b>d</b> STEM-EDS mappings.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The ex situ HAADF-STEM images of the positive electrodes demonstrated a uniform and integral CEI, with a thickness of 5 − 7 nm, that fully covered the cathode surface after the initial cycle (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig4">4b</a>). However, the CEI is susceptible to damage by beam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zachman, M. J., Tu, Z., Choudhury, S., Archer, L. A. &amp; Kourkoutis, L. F. Cryo-STEM mapping of solid–liquid interfaces and dendrites in lithium-metal batteries. Nature 560, 345–349 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1805">42</a></sup>. Cryo-transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) images further determined that the thickness of CEI was 10−14 nm (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig4">4c</a>). The organic species corresponding to amorphous regions ensured a fully passivated interface, and the high-content inorganic components (NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and NaCl) corresponding to areas filled with various lattice fringes provided rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transfer pathways across the interface and remarkable interface robustness. STEM-EDS images further indicated the intensive distribution of F, Cl, Na, and O on the cathode surface (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig4">4d</a>). It effectively facilitated the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer across the interface and interfacial robustness to maintain its integrity during the long-term cycling (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">18</a>), which was also demonstrated by the low activation energy (48.6 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) calculated from the analysis of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements at various temperatures (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">19</a> and Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">5</a>). Considering the contribution of inorganic species to the ionic conductivity and robustness of CEI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jin, Y. et al. Low-solvation electrolytes for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries. Nat. Energy 7, 718–725 (2022)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1834">43</a></sup>, an inorganic-rich CEI well protected the cathode and minimized adverse interfacial reactions, which resulted in a stable interface and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transfer during the long-term cycling of the cell.</p><p>Benefiting from the design of continuous Na<sup>+</sup> channels (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">20</a>), the NFS-H cathode displayed enhanced ion transfer kinetics and reaction reversibility. The voltage profile of the Na||NFS-H coin cell showed an initial discharge capacity of 101.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a discharge cell voltage of 3.75 V at a specific current of 6 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">21</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">4</a>). By contrast, the NFS-L with a less ionic-conducting phase featured a larger hysteresis and a lower initial discharge capacity (89.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at the same specific current. This finding indicated the improved reaction kinetics of the ionic-conductive Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase, which facilitated the decreased electrochemical polarization. Moreover, the low sodium-ion storage capacity of Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> cathode might imply poor Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reversibility (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">9</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Oyama, G., Nishimura, S., Suzuki, Y., Okubo, M. &amp; Yamada, A. Off-stoichiometry in alluaudite-type sodium iron sulfate Na2+2xFe2−x(SO4)3 as an advanced sodium battery cathode material. ChemElectroChem 2, 1019–1023 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1881">29</a></sup>. Thus, the sodium-ion storage capacity of NFS-H was not entirely attributed to the de/intercalation accompanied by Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction in Na<sub>2.26</sub>Fe<sub>1.87</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, but also to minor contributions of the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase. At higher currents (600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), the Na metal cell with the NFS-H-based positive electrode showed a discernable voltage inflexion around 3.0 V in the discharge curves (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">21</a>), which may be associated with the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couple of the Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase in NFS-H<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Oyama, G., Nishimura, S., Suzuki, Y., Okubo, M. &amp; Yamada, A. Off-stoichiometry in alluaudite-type sodium iron sulfate Na2+2xFe2−x(SO4)3 as an advanced sodium battery cathode material. ChemElectroChem 2, 1019–1023 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1921">29</a></sup>. The pairs of weak peaks at 2.89/3.05 and 2.62/2.83 V in the CV curves of Na||NFS-H and Na||NFS-L cells were possibly caused by trace Na<sub>6</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> phase (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">21</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Yao, G. et al. Facile synthesis of hierarchical Na2Fe(SO4)2@rGO/C as high-voltage cathode for energy density-enhanced sodium-ion batteries. J. Energy Chem. 50, 387–394 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1935">39</a></sup>. The sloping voltage curve over the entire range of Na composition in Na||NFS-H and Na||NFS-L cells suggests the (de)sodiation of Na<sup>+</sup> and a single-phase homogeneous reaction mechanism involving minimal volume change (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">21</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chen, M. et al. A novel graphene oxide wrapped Na2Fe2(SO4)3/C cathode composite for long life and high energy density sodium‐ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1800944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1945">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Oyama, G. et al. Sodium intercalation mechanism of 3.8 V class Alluaudite sodium iron sulfate. Chem. Mater. 28, 5321–5328 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1948">44</a></sup>.</p><p>Na metal cells with NFS-H-based positive electrodes displayed excellent rate performance, with discharged capacities of 101.3, 99.7, 98.0, 95.0, 92.1, 88.6, 82.1, and 73.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 6, 12, 24, 60, 120, 240, 600, and 1200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig5">5a</a>). When the specific current returned to 12 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, it retained a high discharged capacity of 97.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which was higher than those obtained for the Na||NFS-L cells. The charge–discharge curves of Na||NFS-H cells at different specific currents exhibited similar voltage plateau and good stability, which indicated the relatively low polarization at high specific currents (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">21</a>). Compared with the reported Fe-based sulfate cathodes, the as-prepared NFS-H exhibited excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity at various rates in laboratory-scale Na metal cell configuration (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">22</a>). The CV curves at different scan rates indicated a capacitive-dominated kinetic process for Na metal cells with the NFS-H cathode (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">22</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">5</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jin, H. et al. Black phosphorus composites with engineered interfaces for high-rate high-capacity lithium storage. Science 370, 192–197 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4816745e1981">45</a></sup>. In addition, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test further determined the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of 110<sup>−12.44</sup> to 10<sup>−10.52</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig5">5</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#Fig5">c</a>), which are comparable to those of its homologs and NASICON-type materials (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-023-39384-7#MOESM1">6</a>);
